[4:2:344,469] independent entities under the California  

[5:0:344,479] Constitution, or are departments with a recognized  

[5:1:344,489] need to operate outside of the administrative  

[5:2:344,499] oversight and control of an agency secretary.  

[5:3:344,509] Constitutionally-established bodies include the  

[5:4:344,519] Legislature, Judicial Branch, Governor's Office,  

[5:5:344,529] and constitutional officers. This section also  

[5:6:344,539] includes such independent entities as the  

[6:0:344,549] Inspector General and the California State Lottery.

[1:0:268,39] 0110   Senate  

[2:0:66,61] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[2:1:66,71] establishing public policy for California. The State Senate is comprised of 40 members who are elected for 4-year terms.  

[2:2:66,81] Members of the Legislature may serve a maximum of 12 years total in the Senate, the Assembly, or both, in any combination of  

[2:3:66,91] terms. Members first elected before June 6, 2012, however, may generally serve a maximum of 14 years total, with no more  

[2:4:66,101] than two terms in the Senate and three terms in the Assembly.

[6:1:66,229] The Legislature is comprised of two houses, the Senate and the Assembly, and is responsible for enacting laws and  

[6:2:66,239] establishing public policy for California. Both houses contribute to support the Legislative Analyst's Office, which provides fiscal  

[6:3:66,250] and policy advice to the Legislature, and is overseen by the 16-member bipartisan Joint Legislative Budget Committee.  

[6:4:66,267] Because the Legislature's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Legislature has a capital outlay program to  

[6:5:66,278] support this need.   For the specifics on the Legislature's   capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[3:0:66,69] The California State Capitol, which serves as the state’s working seat of government, began construction in 1860 and was  

[3:1:66,79] completed in 1874. The Capitol remained largely unchanged until 1949, when additional space requirements drove the need for  

[3:2:66,89] construction of the Capitol Annex, which attaches to the east side of the original Capitol building.   The Annex, which was  

[3:3:66,99] completed in 1951, houses legislative offices, committee rooms, and the Governor’s offices.  

[3:4:66,117] Government Code Section 9112(a) authorizes the Joint Rules Committee to pursue the construction of a new, restored,  

[3:5:66,127] rehabilitated, renovated, or reconstructed capitol building annex and associated projects.

[10:0:139,471] 0150   Contributions to the Legislators' Retirement System  

[10:1:66,492] Established in 1947, the Legislators' Retirement System provides retirement and death benefits for legislators, constitutional  

[10:2:66,502] officers, and legislative statutory officers.  

[11:0:66,523] The California Public Employees' Retirement System (CalPERS) administers the Legislators' Retirement System on behalf of  

[11:1:66,533] its members. Retirement benefits paid are based upon age, years of service, and final compensation. Benefits for death,  

[11:2:66,543] disability, and surviving dependents of deceased members are also provided under specific conditions.   The Legislators'  

[11:3:66,553] Retirement System is funded through contributions from the state, members of the system, and investment earnings. 

[12:0:66,574] Proposition 140, approved by voters in 1990, closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new legislators after November  

[12:1:66,584] 6, 1990.   Subsequently, the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of 2012 (AB 340)  

[12:2:66,594] closed the Legislators' Retirement System to all new members effective January 1, 2013. New constitutional officers, statutory  

[12:3:66,604] officers, Sergeant-at-Arms, and the Insurance Commissioner retain optional membership rights in CalPERS.

[6:0:208,203] 0160   Legislative Counsel Bureau  

[6:1:66,224] The Legislative Counsel Bureau (Bureau) provides legal assistance to the two houses of the Legislature, and their members  

[6:2:66,234] and committees, by resolving a large volume of complex legal problems arising in connection with the legislative process. The  

[6:3:66,244] legal services furnished include rendering opinions, drafting bills, counseling, attending meetings of legislative committees as  

[6:4:66,255] counsel, and representing the Legislature in litigation. The attorney-client relationship is maintained and all work is confidential.  

[7:0:66,275] In addition, the Bureau prepares and provides necessary indices and appropriate tables necessary to identify legislative  

[7:1:66,285] measures and compiles and indexes statutes and codes.  

[8:0:66,306] The Bureau operates the Legislative Data Center, which provides information technology services in support of the legislative  

[8:1:66,316] information system and the processing of legislative measures.  

[9:0:66,336] Pursuant to Chapter 46 of the Statutes of 2012, the California Commission on Uniform State Laws was transferred into the  

[9:1:66,346] Bureau. The California Commission on Uniform State Laws presents to the Legislature uniform laws recommended by the  

[9:2:66,357] National Conference of Commissioners on Uniform State Laws and then promotes passage of these uniform acts.  

[9:3:66,374] Pursuant to Chapter 450 of the Statutes of 2018, the Bureau provides advice and investigation services to the Legislature  

[9:4:66,384] related to workplace misconduct through the Workplace Conduct Unit.

[4:0:242,131] 0250   Judicial Branch  

[5:0:66,153] Article VI of the Constitution creates the Supreme Court of California and the Courts of Appeal to exercise the judicial power of  

[5:1:66,163] the state at the appellate level. Article VI also creates the Judicial Council of California to administer the state's judicial system.  

[5:2:66,173] Chapter 869, Statutes of 1997, created the California Habeas Corpus Resource Center to represent any person financially  

[5:3:66,183] unable to employ appellate counsel in capital cases.  

[6:0:66,204] The Lockyer-Isenberg Trial Court Funding Act of 1997 (Chapter 850, Statutes of 1997) provided a stable and consistent  

[6:1:66,214] funding source for the trial courts. Beginning with fiscal year 1997-98, consolidation of the costs of trial court operation was  

[6:2:66,224] implemented at the state level, with the exception of facility, revenue collection, and local judicial benefit costs. This  

[7:0:66,234] implementation capped the counties' general purpose revenue contributions to trial court costs at a revised 1994-95 level. The  

[7:1:66,245] county contributions become part of the Trial Court Trust Fund, which supports all trial court operations. Fine and penalty  

[8:0:66,255] revenue collected by each county is retained or distributed in accordance with statute. Each county makes quarterly payments  

[8:1:66,265] to the Trial Court Trust Fund equal to the fine and penalty revenue received by the state General Fund in 1994-95, as adjusted  

[8:2:66,275] by amounts equivalent to specified fine and fee revenues that counties benefited from in 2003-04. The Trial Court Facilities Act  

[8:3:66,285] of 2002 (Chapter 1082, statutes of 2002) provided a process for the responsibility for court facilities to be transferred from the  

[8:4:66,296] counties to the state by July 1, 2007, which was extended to December 31, 2009, by Chapter 9, Statutes of 2008. The Trial  

[8:5:66,306] Court Facilities Act of 2002 also established several new revenue sources, effective on January 1, 2003. These revenues are  

[8:6:66,316] deposited into the State Court Facilities Construction Fund for the purpose of funding the construction and maintenance of  

[8:7:66,326] court facilities throughout the state. Counties contribute revenues for the ongoing operation and maintenance of court facilities  

[8:8:66,336] based upon historical expenditures for facilities transferred to the state.  

[9:0:66,357] The mission of the Judicial Branch is to resolve disputes arising under the law and to interpret and apply the law consistently,  

[9:1:66,367] impartially, and independently to protect the rights and liberties guaranteed by the Constitutions of California and the United  

[9:2:66,377] States, in a fair, accessible, effective, and efficient manner.  

[10:0:66,398] Because the Judicial Branch's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Judicial Branch has a capital outlay  

[10:1:66,408] program to support this need. For   the specifics on   the Judicial Branch's capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[10:0:66,409] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[10:1:65,429] • Ongoing Support for the Judicial Branch and Trial Court Operations—The Budget includes $272.2 million ongoing General  

[10:2:76,439] Fund to restore the Judicial Branch to pre-Pandemic funding levels, allowing for courts to re-open temporarily closed  

[10:3:76,449] courtrooms and to resolve case backlogs. It also includes resources to provide the trial courts a 3.7 percent increase in  

[10:4:76,459] funding for general trial court operational costs.  

[11:0:65,475] • Resources to Address Trial Court Backlogs—The Budget includes $60 million one-time General Fund for the trial courts to  

[11:1:76,486] specifically address backlogs and workload delays resulting from the COVID-19 Pandemic.  

[11:2:65,502] • Early Disposition Readiness Conference Program—The Budget includes $30 million one-time General Fund to expedite  

[11:3:76,512] adjudications by making temporary assigned judges available to complete readiness conferences to resolve low-level  

[11:4:76,522] misdemeanors or felony cases before going to trial, thereby reducing the backlog of criminal cases and providing victims and  

[11:5:76,532] offenders a more timely resolution.  

[12:0:65,549] • Trial Court Trust Fund Revenue Backfill—The Budget includes $118.3 million General Fund to continue backfilling the Trial  

[12:1:76,559] Court Trust Fund for a decline in revenues expected in 2021-22.  

[13:0:65,575] • Facility Operations and Maintenance—The Budget includes $53.5 million ongoing General Fund to support maintenance,  

[13:1:76,585] utility, and lease costs for trial courts and the Courts of Appeal facilities.  

[14:0:65,601] • One-time Deferred Maintenance—The Budget includes $188.1 million one-time General Fund to support deferred  

[14:1:76,612] maintenance projects in trial courts and Courts of Appeal.  

[15:0:65,628] • Statewide Expansion of Pretrial Decisions—The Budget includes $140 million General Fund in 2021-22 and $70 million  

[15:1:76,638] ongoing to promote the safe reduction in pretrial detention by supporting pretrial release decisions that impose the least  

[15:2:76,648] restrictive conditions while maintaining public safety.  

[16:0:65,664] • Legal Aid Augmentation—The Budget includes $50 million General Fund in 2021-22 and $20 million in 2022-23 and ongoing  

[16:1:76,675] for legal aid services funded through the Equal Access Fund for low-income litigants.  

[17:0:65,691] • Legal Aid for Renters in Landlord-Tenant Disputes and Homeowners to Avoid Foreclosure—The Budget includes $40 million  

[17:1:76,701] federal American Rescue Plan Act of 2021 funds in 2021-22, and $20 million annually thereafter until 2023-24 ($80 million

# === PAGE 26 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — LJE   24 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:207,32] 0250   Judicial Branch - Continued 

[3:0:66,66] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,85] 0130 - SUPREME COURT  

[4:0:66,105] The Supreme Court is the highest court in the California judicial system. Its decisions are binding on all other California state  

[4:1:66,115] courts. The Chief Justice of California and the six Associate Justices entertain petitions seeking review of decisions from the  

[4:2:66,126] Courts of Appeal, original petitions for extraordinary relief (such as writs of mandate or habeas corpus), and recommendations  

[4:3:66,136] for discipline of judicial officers and attorneys. The Court grants review and issues opinions in order to settle legal questions of  

[4:4:66,146] statewide importance. In addition, under the California Constitution, all death penalty judgments are appealed directly to the  

[4:5:66,156] Supreme Court.  

[4:6:66,174] 0135 - COURTS OF APPEAL  

[5:0:66,194] Established by a constitutional amendment in 1904, the Courts of Appeal are California's intermediate courts of review. The six  

[5:1:66,205] District Courts of Appeal hear appeals and original proceedings at nine different locations around the state. Cases before the  

[5:2:66,215] Courts of Appeal involve every area of civil and criminal law.  

[5:3:66,232] 0140 - JUDICIAL COUNCIL  

[6:0:66,253] The Judicial Council of California is the constitutional policy-making body for the state judiciary. The Council consists of 21  

[6:1:66,263] voting members and 9 advisory members; the Chief Justice of California serves as chair. The Judicial Council staff serve as the  

[6:2:66,273] administrative arm of the Council. Staff provide policy support to the Council, administrative accountability in the operation of  

[6:3:66,283] the courts as specified by law, strategic planning for capital outlay, design, and construction of court facilities; and  

[6:4:66,294] administrative support for courts in areas such as budget, fiscal services, coordination of the assignment of retired judges,  

[6:5:66,304] technology, education, legal advice and services, human resources, legislative advocacy, and research.  

[6:6:66,322] 0140023 - JUDICIAL BRANCH FACILITY PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,339] The Judicial Branch Facility Program administers the acquisition, planning, construction, operations, and maintenance of  

[7:1:66,349] judicial branch facilities. This program is responsible for the development of long-term facilities master plans, facility and real  

[7:2:66,360] estate management, renovation of existing court facilities, and new courthouse planning, design, and construction.  

[7:3:66,377] 0150 - STATE TRIAL COURT FUNDING  

[8:0:66,395] California has 58 trial courts, one in each county. In trial courts, or superior courts, a judge and sometimes a jury hears  

[8:1:66,405] witnesses' testimony and other evidence and decides cases by applying the relevant law to the facts.   Trial courts have trial  

[8:2:66,415] jurisdiction over all criminal and civil cases. To accomplish various statutory mandates, the State Trial Court Funding Program  

[8:3:66,426] supports trial courts through the following programs:  

[8:4:66,446] 0150010 - SUPPORT FOR THE OPERATION OF THE TRIAL COURTS  

[8:5:66,464] This program's objective is to provide the resources necessary for the statewide trial court system to adjudicate civil and  

[8:6:66,474] criminal cases. This program includes all allowable trial court administrative costs under Government Code section 77003.  

[8:7:66,492] 0150011 - COURT APPOINTED DEPENDENCY COUNSEL  

[9:0:66,509] This program provides funding for court-appointed counsel for children, non-minor dependents and parents in juvenile court  

[9:1:66,520] dependency proceedings pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 317, subdivisions (b) and (c), and Government  

[9:2:66,530] Code section 77003, subdivision (a)(4).  

[9:3:66,547] 0150019 - COMPENSATION OF SUPERIOR COURT JUDGES  

[9:4:66,565] This program provides funding for the salaries and state benefits for Superior Court judges.  

[9:5:66,583] 0150028 - ASSIGNED JUDGES  

[10:0:66,601] This program provides support for the salaries and related costs of retired as well as active judges who are assigned by the  

[10:1:66,611] Chief Justice to positions in courts which require assistance due to judicial absences or other factors impacting the ability of a  

[10:2:66,621] court to avoid case delay.  

[10:3:66,639] 0150037 - COURT INTERPRETERS  

[11:0:66,656] This program supports the provision of qualified language interpreters in criminal, juvenile, family and civil proceedings as  

[11:1:66,667] required by statute.  

[11:2:66,684] 0150046 - GRANTS  

[12:0:66,702] This program provides funding for government grants providing public services related to the trial courts.

[3:0:66,69] 0150051 – CHILD SUPPORT COMMISSIONER PROGRAM (AB 1058)  

[3:1:66,86] This program provides funding for Child Support Commissioners and Family Law Facilitators in every court.  

[3:2:66,104] 0150067 – COURT APPOINTED SPECIAL ADVOCATE (CASA) PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,122] This program provides funding for non-profits designated by the court to train and provide volunteers to advocate for children in  

[4:1:66,132] foster care.  

[4:2:66,150] 0150083 – EQUAL ACCESS FUND  

[5:0:66,167] This program provides funding for legal services programs to provide a range of services to low-income litigants.  

[5:1:66,185] 0150095 - EXPENSES ON BEHALF OF THE TRIAL COURT  

[6:0:66,203] This program provides appropriation for statewide programs and services provided to the court or courts by the Judicial Council  

[6:1:66,213] or payment for services or property of any kind contracted for the court or courts or on behalf of the courts by the Judicial  

[6:2:66,223] Council, which are paid for or reimbursed by the trial courts. The appropriation also supports the implementation and  

[6:3:66,233] administration of the Civil Representation Pilot Program.  

[7:0:66,251] In addition to the programs mentioned above, the State Trial Court Funding Program supports a number of smaller programs  

[7:1:66,261] including Access to Visitation services in family law proceedings, JusticeCorps volunteers in the courts, and Substance Abuse  

[7:2:66,271] Focus Grants supporting collaborative justice courts.  

[7:3:66,289] 0155 - HABEAS CORPUS RESOURCE CENTER  

[8:0:66,310] The Habeas Corpus Resource Center provides legal representation for indigent petitioners in death penalty habeas corpus  

[8:1:66,320] proceedings before the California state courts   and the federal courts. The Center also recruits and trains attorneys to expand  

[8:2:66,330] the pool of private counsel qualified to accept appointments in death penalty habeas corpus proceedings, serves as a resource  

[8:3:66,340] to them, and thereby helps to reduce the number of unrepresented indigents on California's death row.

[11:0:66,527] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[12:0:66,546] The Judicial Branch consists of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, trial courts, and the Judicial Council. The Supreme Court  

[12:1:66,556] is located in the 103,300 square foot (sf) Civic Center Plaza in San Francisco and the 7,600 sf Ronald Reagan State Building in  

[12:2:66,567] Los Angeles. The courts of appeal are organized into six districts, operate in nine different locations, and consist of  

[12:3:66,577] approximately 508,000 sf. The trial courts are located in 58 counties statewide, in approximately 450 facilities, and 2,100  

[12:4:66,587] courtrooms of approximately 16 million sf of usable area and more than 21 million sf of space under Judicial Council  

[12:5:66,597] responsibility and management. The facilities of the Supreme Court, courts of appeal, and trial courts encompass not only the  

[12:6:66,607] public courtroom spaces but also the chambers and workspaces where judicial officers and courtroom staff prepare for  

[12:7:66,618] proceedings; secure areas, including holding cells; and building support functions. Judicial Council administrative facilities,  

[12:8:66,628] occupying approximately 250,000 sf, are located in San Francisco and Sacramento.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[10:0:180,488] 0280   Commission on Judicial Performance  

[11:0:66,509] The California Commission on Judicial Performance is the independent state agency responsible for investigating complaints of  

[11:1:66,519] judicial misconduct and judicial incapacity, and for disciplining judges pursuant to Article VI, Section 18 of the California  

[11:2:66,530] Constitution. It is the only body with such authority in the state. Its jurisdiction includes all active judges and justices of  

[11:3:66,540] California's superior courts, Courts of Appeal and Supreme Court, and former judges for conduct prior to retirement or  

[11:4:66,550] resignation. The Commission also shares authority with the local courts for the oversight of court commissioners and referees.  

[11:5:66,560] In addition to its disciplinary functions, the Commission is responsible for handling judges' applications for disability retirement.  

[12:0:66,581] The Commission's authority is limited to investigating alleged judicial misconduct and, if warranted, imposing discipline. Judicial  

[12:1:66,591] misconduct usually involves conduct in conflict with the standards set forth in the Code of Judicial Ethics. After investigation  

[12:2:66,601] and, in some cases a public hearing, the Commission may impose sanctions ranging from confidential discipline to removal  

[12:3:66,611] from office.  

[13:0:66,632] The Commission is composed of 11 members: 3 judges appointed by the Supreme Court; 2 attorneys appointed by the  

[13:1:66,642] Governor; and 6 lay citizens, of which 2 are appointed by the Governor, 2 are appointed by the Senate Committee on Rules,  

[13:2:66,652] and 2 are appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. Members are appointed to four-year terms and may serve two terms.  

[13:3:66,662] Commission members do not receive a salary.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:150,155] 0390   Contributions to the Judges' Retirement System  

[6:0:66,177] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement benefit funding for judges on California's Supreme, Appellate, and  

[6:1:66,187] Superior/Municipal courts. The California Public Employees' Retirement System administers both the Judges' Retirement  

[6:2:66,197] System and the Judges' Retirement System II on behalf of its members.  

[7:0:66,215] The Judges' Retirement System is closed to new members, and a second retirement system for judges was established in  

[7:1:66,225] 1994. All new judges elected or appointed on or after November 9, 1994 become members of the Judges' Retirement System  

[7:2:66,235] II. The Judges' Retirement System II provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service, final  

[7:3:66,245] compensation, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement System II Law. Judges' Retirement  

[7:4:66,256] System II members eligible for a service retirement have the option of choosing the monetary credit plan (a lump-sum return of  

[7:5:66,266] contributions and interest earned) or the defined benefit plan. Judges' Retirement System II members not eligible for a defined  

[7:6:66,276] benefit receive the balance of their monetary credits (in a lump-sum payment). The Judges' Retirement System II receives  

[7:7:66,286] contributions from judges equal to 8 percent of salary as well as any investment income. The state's contributions are adjusted  

[7:8:66,296] annually to maintain actuarial soundness of the fund.  

[8:0:66,317] The Judges' Retirement System provides retirement, disability, and death benefits based on age, years of service,  

[9:0:66,327] compensation of active judges, and eligibility, as determined by specific sections of the Judges' Retirement Law. Active judges  

[9:1:66,337] and the state each contribute 8 percent of salary to the Judges' Retirement System. Additional contributions come from filing  

[9:2:66,347] fees for specific civil cases and investment income. These contributions, however, are not sufficient to fully fund benefit  

[9:3:66,358] payments. Consequently, current law requires the state to fund the difference between existing contribution sources and the  

[9:4:66,368] required benefit payments to retired judges.  

[10:0:66,388] Effective January 1, 2013, provisions of the California Public Employees' Pension Reform Act of 2013, Chapter 296, Statutes of  

[10:1:66,398] 2012 (AB 340), require that new judges contribute 50 percent of the normal cost of their pension benefits.

[5:0:65,152] •  California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank)— The Budget includes $50 million one-time General

[7:1:76,246] 2021-22 to implement strategic media recovery campaigns that will jump start the recovery of the travel and tourism industry.  

[7:2:76,256] The California Office of Tourism, within GO-Biz, will coordinate with Visit California to implement this initiative.

[8:2:76,292] is in addition to the $15.5 million ongoing Cannabis Tax Fund allocated to GO-Biz for this purpose, bringing the 2021-22 total  

[8:3:76,303] to $35.5 million.  

[9:0:65,319] •  Zero Emission Vehicle Package: Market Development Strategy— The Budget includes $5 million one-time General Fund  

[9:1:76,329] for GO-Biz as part of a $3.9 billion package to equitably scale the zero emission vehicle market and accelerate the state  

[9:2:76,339] toward meeting its climate and transportation goals. This includes funding to support: ZEV Market Development Strategy.  

[10:0:65,355] •  Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package: Climate Catalyst Fund— Chapter 14, Statutes of 2021 (SB 85) provided $16  

[10:1:76,366] million one-time General Fund in 2020-21 as a part of a comprehensive package to increase the pace and scale of forest  

[10:2:76,376] health activities and reduce wildfire risk. In total, the Budget includes a $1.5 billion wildfire prevention and forest resilience  

[10:3:76,386] investment over three years ($536 million in 2020 21, $458 million in 2021-22, and $500 million in 2022?23). Allocations for  

[10:4:76,396] the 2021-22 and 2022-23 budgets will be determined in subsequent legislation.  

[11:0:65,412] •  Inclusive Innovation Hub (iHub 2 

[11:1:205,416] ) Program— The Budget includes $2.5 million one-time General Fund in 2021-22 to restart  

[11:2:76,427] and expand upon the Innovation Hub Program established in Chapter 530, Statutes of 2013. This program will incentivize a  

[11:3:76,437] focus on diversity, equity, and inclusion in the state’s tech and science-based startup ecosystem.  

[12:0:65,453] •  Energy Unit— The Budget includes $950,000 ongoing General Fund to establish an Energy Unit to provide dedicated

[12:2:76,473] mitigation.

[3:3:66,99] assistance and helping businesses succeed in California.  

[4:0:66,117] 0230 - CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF THE SMALL BUSINESS ADVOCATE  

[5:0:66,135] The California Office of the Small Business Advocate (CalOSBA) serves as the principal advocate in the state on behalf of  

[5:1:66,145] small businesses, including legislation and administrative regulations that affect small business. CalOSBA ensures that  

[5:2:66,155] information, content and direct support are available to minority and small businesses to better navigate state resources,  

[5:3:66,165] programs and services. CalOSBA advocates on behalf of small businesses concerning the actions of state agencies, state  

[5:4:66,175] laws, regulations and procurement opportunities, maintaining the connection to agency small business advocates/liaisons. It  

[5:5:66,186] serves as the co-Coordinator with CalOES for the Economic Recovery Support Function under the California Disaster  

[5:6:66,196] Recovery Framework. CalOSBA administers the Technical Assistance Expansion Program and Capital Infusion Program grants  

[5:7:66,206] for support services to underserved business groups, as well as the Innovation Hub (iHub) and CA Made programs.  

[6:0:66,224] 0235010 - CALIFORNIA FILM COMMISSION  

[7:0:66,241] The California Film Commission (CFC) enhances California's status as the leader in motion picture, television and commercial  

[7:1:66,252] production. The Commission is tasked with retaining and increasing motion picture production and to see that it continues to  

[7:2:66,262] create jobs and boost business throughout the State. A one-stop office for filmmakers, the Commission supports productions of  

[7:3:66,272] all sizes and budgets with a variety of services. In addition to issuing film permits for all state properties, administering the film  

[7:4:66,282] and TV tax credit program, maintaining an extensive location library, and offering production assistance on a wide variety of  

[7:5:66,292] issues, CFC also works closely with cities and counties with the goal of creating 'film friendly" policies that are consistent state-

[7:6:66,303] wide.  

[8:0:66,320] 0235019 - DIVISION OF TOURISM  

[9:0:66,338] The California Tourism Market Act provides for the marketing of California through an assessment of businesses that benefit  

[9:1:66,348] from travel and tourism. The objective of the Tourism Assessment Program is to identify potentially assessable businesses,  

[9:2:66,358] assist companies with determining the appropriate amount of their self-assessment, and collet the fee.  

[10:0:66,376] 0235028 - CALIFORINIA INFRASTRUCTURE AND ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENT BANK  

[11:0:66,394] California Infrastructure and Economic Development Bank (IBank) was created to finance public infrastructure and private  

[11:1:66,404] development that promote a healthy climate for jobs, contribute to a strong economy and improve the quality of life in California  

[11:2:66,414] communities. IBank has broad authority to participate in a variety of financing transactions, including issuing tax-exempt and  

[11:3:66,424] taxable revenue bonds, providing financing to a variety of entities, providing credit enhancements, acquiring or leasing facilities,  

[11:4:66,435] and leveraging State and Federal funds. IBank's current programs include the infrastructure state revolving fund program (ISRF  

[11:5:66,445] Program), the California lending for energy and environmental needs center (CLEEN Center, a sub-program of the ISRF  

[11:6:66,455] Program), the statewide energy and efficiency program and the light emitting diode street lighting program (each a component  

[11:7:66,465] of the CLEEN Center), the bond financing program, which issues 501(c)(3)tax-exempt and taxable bonds,, industrial  

[11:8:66,475] development revenue bonds, exempt facility revenue bonds, and public agency revenue bonds.  

[12:0:66,493] 0235037 - SMALL BUSINESS LOAN GUARANTEE PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,511] The Small Business Loan Guarantee Program (SBLGP) promotes local economic development by providing guarantees for  

[13:1:66,521] loans issued to small businesses from financial institutions, typically banks, that otherwise would not approve a term loan or  

[13:2:66,531] line of credit to a small business. As a result of the SBLGP, participating small businesses are able to secure financing that  

[13:3:66,541] allows them to grow and expand their business. The loan guarantee serves as a credit enhancement and an incentive for  

[13:4:66,552] financial institutions to make loans to small businesses that otherwise would not be eligible for such financing.  

[14:0:66,569] 0235046 - CALIFORNIA WELCOME CENTERS  

[15:0:66,587] California Welcome Centers are visitor information centers that are readily accessible to and recognizable by tourists to  

[15:1:66,597] encourage tourism in California and provide benefits to the state economy. The objective of the California Welcome Center  

[15:2:66,607] Program is to determine the locality of underserved travelers, designate a welcome center, and establish operating standards  

[15:3:66,618] across the network.  

[16:0:66,635] 0240 - CALIFORNIA COMMUNITY REINVESTMENT GRANT PROGRAM 

[17:0:66,653] The California Community Reinvestment Grants (CalCRG) Program   was established on July 1, 2018   to   implement the  

[17:1:66,663] competitive grant program mandated by Proposition 64, the Adult Use of Marijuana Act, and the provisions specified in  

[17:2:66,673] Revenue and Taxation Code section 34019(d).   The CalCRG Program administers grants   for   local public   health departments  

[17:3:66,684] and qualified community-based nonprofit organizations to support various mental health treatment, substance use treatment,  

[17:4:66,694] job placement, legal services and other health and wellness-related programs for communities disproportionately affected by  

[17:5:66,704] past federal and state drug policies in California. GO-Biz must award grants annually and at least 50 percent of the grant  

[17:6:66,714] funding will be allocated to qualified community-based nonprofit organizations.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:157,192] 0511   Secretary for Government Operations Agency  

[6:0:66,213] The Government Operations Agency is responsible for coordinating and overseeing state operations, including procurement,  

[6:1:66,223] information technology, and human resources. The mission of the Government Operations Agency is to improve management  

[6:2:66,233] and accountability of government programs, increase efficiency, and promote better and more coordinated operational  

[6:3:66,244] decisions. The Government Operations Agency oversees the Office of Administrative Law, California Public Employees'  

[6:4:66,254] Retirement System, California State Teachers' Retirement System, State Personnel Board, Victim Compensation Board,  

[6:5:66,264] Department of General Services, Department of Technology, Department of Human Resources, Franchise Tax Board, and  

[6:6:66,274] Department of Tax and Fee Administration. In addition, the Government Operations Agency administers the California  

[6:7:66,284] Complete Count-Census 2020 Office, Office of Digital Innovation, Cradle-to-Career Data System, and Racial Equity Advisory  

[6:8:66,295] Council.

[6:0:99,292] 0515   Secretary for Business, Consumer Services, and Housing Agency  

[7:0:66,314] The Business, Consumer Services, and Housing Agency (Agency) is responsible for overseeing departments, boards,  

[8:0:66,324] commissions, and agencies that provide the following services to the people of California:   protection of the public through the  

[8:1:66,334] licensing and regulation of more than 100 businesses and 200 professional categories; regulation of financial services and  

[8:2:66,344] state-licensed financial institutions; enforcement of the sale of alcoholic beverages; regulation of the horse racing industry;  

[8:3:66,355] protection of civil rights; homelessness prevention through the coordination of funding and services; and safe and affordable  

[9:0:66,365] housing opportunities.   The Agency is comprised of the following entities:   the Department of Consumer Affairs, the Department  

[9:1:66,375] of Real Estate, the Department of Housing and Community Development, the Department of Fair Employment and Housing,  

[9:2:66,385] the Department of Financial Protection and Innovation, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, the Alcoholic Beverage  

[9:3:66,395] Control Appeals Board, the California Housing Finance Agency, the California Horse Racing Board, and the Cannabis Control  

[9:4:66,406] Appeals Panel.   In addition, the Agency is charged with administration of the Homeless Coordinating and Financing Council  

[10:0:66,416] and is proposed to oversee the newly formed Department of Cannabis Control beginning in 2021-22.   Due to recent passage of  

[10:1:66,426] Proposition 24, the new California Privacy Protection Agency will be housed under the Agency.

[4:0:66,135] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[4:1:65,155] • Homeless Landscape Assessment—The Budget includes $5.6 million one-time General Fund for the Homeless Coordinating  

[4:2:76,165] and Financing Council (HCFC) to contract with a vendor to conduct an analysis of the homelessness service providers and  

[4:3:76,175] programs at the local and state level. 

[5:0:65,191] • Homeless Data Integration System—The Budget includes $4 million one-time General Fund to support the state’s Homeless  

[5:1:76,202] Data Integration System. 

[6:0:65,218] • Family Homelessness Challenge Grants and Technical Assistance—The Budget includes $40 million one-time General Fund  

[6:1:76,228] to provide grants and technical assistance to local jurisdictions to develop action plans that will address family homelessness  

[6:2:76,238] and accelerate local jurisdictions’ rehousing efforts. 

[6:3:65,254] • Encampment Clean-up—The Budget includes $50.3 million one-time General Fund for HCFC to partner with a selection of  

[6:4:76,265] local governments and assist them with resolving critical encampments and transitioning individuals living on site into  

[6:5:76,275] permanent housing.  

[7:0:65,291] • Flexible Local Aid—The Budget includes $1 billion in 2021-22 and an additional $1 billion in 2022-23 for flexible local  

[7:1:76,301] homelessness funding, with robust oversight and accountability requirements.

[3:1:65,88] • Transportation Infrastructure Package—The Budget includes $2 billion to deliver priority transit and rail projects statewide  

[3:2:76,98] including projects that will be completed in time for the 2028 Olympic Games in Los Angeles.   Additionally, the budget  

[3:3:76,109] provides $500 million to support high priority grade separations and grade crossing improvement, and $280 million for  

[3:4:76,119] improvements that facilitate freight and passenger access and promote the efficient and safe movement of goods and  

[3:5:76,129] people.

[4:1:76,155] lease state-of-the-art, clean bus and rail equipment and infrastructure that eliminate fossil fuel emissions and increase  

[4:2:76,166] intercity rail and intercity bus frequencies.

[11:1:66,564] departments, board and programs it oversees, to reduce duplication and fragmentation among HHSA departments in policy  

[11:2:66,574] development and implementation, to improve coordination among departments on common programs, to ensure programmatic  

[11:3:66,584] integrity, and to advance the Governor's priorities on health and human services issues.  

[12:0:66,604] The HHSA accomplishes its mission through the administration and coordination of state and federal programs for public  

[12:1:66,615] health, health care services, social services, public assistance, health planning and licensing, and rehabilitation. These  

[13:0:66,625] programs touch the lives of millions of California's most needy and vulnerable residents. The HHSA is committed to striking a  

[13:1:66,635] balance between maintaining access to essential health and human services for California's most disadvantaged and at-risk  

[13:2:66,645] residents while pursuing ways to better manage and control costs.  

[14:0:66,666] The following departments and entities fall under the purview of the HHSA:  

[14:1:65,683] • Department of Aging  

[15:0:65,699] • Department of Child Support Services

[3:0:66,69] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[3:1:65,88] • The Budget would consolidate existing resources to establish a Center for Data Insights and Innovation within the Agency,  

[3:2:76,98] contingent upon passage of future legislation. The Center would focus on leveraging data to develop knowledge and insights  

[3:3:76,109] to improve program delivery and drive system transformation across health and human services.  

[3:4:65,125] • The Budget includes $7.6 million General Fund in 2021-22 and $7.2 million ongoing General Fund for 33 positions to stand  

[3:5:76,135] up the Office of Youth and Community Restoration and perform the core functions laid out in SB 823 (Chapter 337, Statutes  

[3:6:76,145] of 2020), including: (1) review of county’s juvenile justice plans, (2) reporting on youth outcomes and identifying best  

[3:7:76,155] practices, (3) providing technical assistance to counties, and (4) performing the ombudsperson duties. The Budget also  

[3:8:76,166] includes $20 million General Fund in 2021-22 to provide technical assistance, disseminate best practices, and issue grants  

[3:9:76,176] to counties and probation departments for the purpose of transforming the juvenile justice system to improve outcomes for  

[3:10:76,186] justice involved youth.  

[4:0:65,202] • The Budget includes resources for equity-centered programs, including: $1.7 million General Fund for the Agency to conduct  

[4:1:76,212] a retrospective analysis of COVID-19, health disparities and health equity to help inform future response; $3.2 million  

[4:2:76,223] General Fund to develop an equity dashboard to identify data completeness, disparities, disproportionalities, and program  

[4:3:76,233] participation for Health and Human Services programs; $2.5 million General Fund to support the CALeads initiative to  

[4:4:76,243] diversify the state workforce within California Health and Human Services departments; and $20.3 million General Fund to  

[4:5:76,253] provide cross-departmental technical assistance and support the development and implementation of an Agency-wide  

[4:6:76,263] language access policy and process framework.  

[5:0:65,280] • The Budget includes $35.1 million General Fund ongoing to support public education, coordination, subject matter expertise,  

[5:1:76,290] and evaluation activities for the Children and Youth Behavioral Health Initiative.  

[5:2:65,306] • The Budget includes $2.5 million General Fund in 2021-22 to facilitate health and social service program information  

[5:3:76,316] exchange activities, contingent upon passage of future legislation.

[5:0:66,218] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,237] 0280 - SECRETARY FOR HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES  

[6:0:66,257] The Secretary for California Health and Human Services Agency provides the Governor with the highest level of advice on  

[6:1:66,268] state health, human services and related budget policy issues. The Secretary also provides the highest level of leadership and  

[6:2:66,278] oversight of the agency-wide efforts to promote the health and well-being of a growing and increasingly diverse California  

[6:3:66,288] population.  

[7:0:66,306] 0285 - THE CALIFORNIA OFFICE OF HEALTH INFORMATION INTEGRITY  

[8:0:66,326] The California Office of Health Information Integrity (CalOHII) is responsible for statewide leadership, coordination, policy  

[8:1:66,336] formation, direction, and oversight responsibilities for federal Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA)  

[8:2:66,346] requirements. As the leading resource for providing HIPAA expertise to the State of California, CalOHII exercises full authority  

[8:3:66,357] relative to state entities to establish policy, provide direction to state entities, monitor progress and report on implementation  

[8:4:66,367] efforts based on state and federal laws.  

[8:5:66,385] 0286 - OFFICE OF YOUTH AND COMMUNITY RESTORATION  

[9:0:66,402] The mission for the Office of Youth and Community Restoration Community is to support the transition of justice involved youth  

[9:1:66,412] to be served in local communities and promote trauma responsive, culturally informed services for youth involved in the juvenile  

[9:2:66,423] justice system that support the youths’ successful transition into adulthood and help them become responsible, thriving, and  

[9:3:66,433] engaged members of their communities.  

[9:4:66,451] 0290 - OFFICE OF SYSTEMS INTEGRATION  

[10:0:66,471] The Office of Systems Integration's (OSI) mission is to procure, manage, and deliver complex technology systems that support  

[10:1:66,481] the delivery of health and human services for automation projects for the Health and Human Services Agency, the Department  

[10:2:66,491] of Social Services, the Department of Health Care Services, the Department of Public Health, and the Health Benefit  

[10:3:66,502] Exchange, including: 

[10:4:65,519] • Appeals Case Management System  

[11:0:65,535] • California Healthcare Eligibility, Enrollment and Retention System (CalHEERS)  

[11:1:65,552] • Case Management, Information and Payrolling System II (CMIPS)  

[11:2:65,568] • Child Welfare Services/Case Management System (CWS/CMS)  

[11:3:65,584] • Child Welfare Digital Services (CWDS)  

[12:0:65,600] • Child Welfare Services - California Automated Response and Engagement System  

[12:1:65,616] • Electronic Benefit Transfer System (EBT)  

[13:0:65,633] • Electronic Women, Infants and Children Management Information System Project (eWIC)  

[13:1:65,649] • Medi-Cal Eligibility Data System Modernization (MEDS Mod)  

[13:2:65,665] • Statewide Automated Welfare System (SAWS)  

[13:3:65,681] • Welfare Data Tracking Implementation Project (WDTIP)

[6:0:164,330] 0540   Secretary of the Natural Resources Agency  

[7:0:66,352] The mission of the Natural Resources Agency is to restore, protect and manage the state's natural, historical and cultural  

[7:1:66,362] resources for current and future generations using creative approaches and solutions based on science, collaboration and  

[7:2:66,372] respect for all involved communities. The Secretary for Natural Resources, a member of the Governor's Cabinet, sets the  

[7:3:66,382] policies and coordinates the environmental preservation and restoration activities of 26 various departments, boards,  

[8:0:66,392] commissions and conservancies, and directly administers the Sea Grant Program, Ocean Protection Council, California  

[8:1:66,403] Environmental Quality Act, Environmental Enhancement Mitigation Program, River Parkways, Urban Greening, and the  

[8:2:66,413] California Cultural and Historical Endowment grant programs.  

[9:0:66,433] The Natural Resources Agency consists of the departments of Forestry and Fire Protection, Conservation, Fish and Wildlife,  

[9:1:66,443] Parks and Recreation, and Water Resources; the California Conservation Corps; Exposition Park; California Science Center;  

[9:2:66,454] California African American Museum; the State Lands Commission; the Colorado River Board; the San Francisco Bay  

[10:0:66,464] Conservation and Development Commission; the Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission; the Wildlife  

[10:1:66,474] Conservation Board; the Delta Protection Commission; the California Coastal Commission; the State Coastal Conservancy; the  

[10:2:66,484] California Tahoe Conservancy; the Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy; the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy; the  

[10:3:66,494] San Joaquin River Conservancy; the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy; the Baldwin  

[10:4:66,505] Hills Conservancy; the San Diego River Conservancy; the Sierra Nevada Conservancy; the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta  

[10:5:66,515] Conservancy; the Native American Heritage Commission; and the Special Resources Program.

[7:0:66,500] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[7:1:65,520] • Water Resilience Package—The Budget includes $6.3 million one-time General Fund as part of a $5.1 billion package to  

[7:2:76,530] support water resilience and drought response efforts. This includes funding to support rehabilitation strategies at Clear Lake  

[7:3:76,540] as well as coordinating immediate drought response efforts.  

[8:0:65,556] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package—Chapter 14, Statutes of 2021 (SB 85) provided $536 million one-time General Fund  

[8:1:76,566] in 2020-21 for a comprehensive package to increase the pace and scale of forest health activities and reduce wildfire risk. In  

[8:2:76,577] total, the Budget includes a $1.5 billion wildfire prevention and forest resilience investment over three years ($536 million in  

[8:3:76,587] 2020 21, $458 million in 2021-22, and $500 million in 2022 23). Allocations for the 2021-22 and 2022-23 budgets will be  

[8:4:76,597] determined in subsequent legislation.  

[9:0:65,613] • Museum Grant Program—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund for projects and programs supporting and  

[9:1:76,623] enhancing museum services and serving historically underserved communities at the California Cultural and Historical  

[9:2:76,634] Endowment.  

[10:0:65,650] • Presidio Improvement Projects—The Budget includes $27.5 million one-time General Fund to support expanding and  

[10:1:76,660] enhancing equitable access to the outdoors through increased seating, improved public transit access, and new paths and  

[10:2:76,670] bikeways.  

[11:0:65,686] • Investments to Accelerate Progress on the State’s Clean Energy Goals—The Budget includes $812 million to accelerate  

[11:1:76,697] California’s progress on meeting its clean energy goals and better position California as a leader in advancing clean  

[11:2:76,707] technologies. This includes $2 million one-time General Fund to the Ocean Protection Council to support research

[6:0:66,221] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,240] The California Natural Resources Agency has one Capital Outlay project, overseeing the Species Conservation Habitat Project  

[7:1:66,250] at the Salton Sea. The Project includes development of a series of ponds, sediment mixing basins, ditches, and berms allowing  

[7:2:66,261] water currently detached from the sea to be managed to provide suitable habitat for fish and birds. The Project is one  

[7:3:66,271] component of the Salton Sea Management Plan.

[14:0:199,618] 0552   Office of the Inspector General  

[14:1:66,640] The Office of the Inspector General (OIG) protects public safety by safeguarding the integrity of California's correctional  

[14:2:66,650] system. OIG is responsible for contemporaneous oversight of the California Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation's  

[14:3:66,660] (CDCR) internal affairs investigations, use of force, staff complaints, and the employee disciplinary process. The Inspector  

[14:4:66,670] General audits and reviews the policies, practices, and procedures of CDCR. The Inspector General reviews the Governor's  

[14:5:66,681] candidates for appointment to serve as warden for the state's adult correctional institutions and as superintendents for the  

[14:6:66,691] state's juvenile facilities, and conducts metric-oriented inspection programs to periodically review delivery of medical care at  

[14:7:66,701] each state prison. OIG receives communications from individuals alleging improper governmental activity and maintains a toll-

[14:8:66,711] free public telephone number to receive allegations of wrongdoing by employees of CDCR; conducts formal reviews of  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:175,69] 0555   Secretary for Environmental Protection  

[2:0:66,91] The Secretary for Environmental Protection is the cabinet-level agency responsible for protecting the environment. California  

[3:0:66,101] Environmental Protection Agency programs reduce greenhouse gas emissions, restore and protect environmental quality, and  

[3:1:66,111] protect public health. The Secretary coordinates the state's environmental regulatory programs and oversees the fair and  

[4:0:66,121] consistent enforcement of environmental law, which safeguards the state's residents and promotes the state's economic vitality.  

[4:1:66,131] The Secretary also administers environmental justice, environmental law enforcement, emergency preparedness and  

[5:0:66,142] response, Certified Unified Program Agencies, and scientific review programs. The Secretary leads greenhouse gas emission  

[5:1:66,152] reduction and climate change activities in state government, and is responsible for coordinating the implementation of Chapter  

[5:2:66,162] 488, Statutes of 2006 (AB 32). The Secretary also centralizes coordination of California-Mexico border environmental efforts.

[7:0:66,363] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,382] 0340 - SUPPORT PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,402] The Secretary for Environmental Protection's primary program objectives are restoring, protecting, and enhancing  

[8:1:66,413] environmental quality, and protecting public health while fostering economic vitality. The Secretary oversees the state's  

[8:2:66,423] environmental regulatory programs with particular emphasis on promoting "cross-media" coordination, enhancing risk  

[8:3:66,433] assessment (especially for disadvantaged communities and other sensitive populations), enforcing laws consistently and fairly,  

[8:4:66,443] and objectively measuring and reporting results.

[6:0:195,288] 0650   Office of Planning and Research  

[7:0:66,309] The Office of Planning and Research (OPR) assists the Governor and the Administration in planning, research, policy  

[8:0:66,319] development, and legislative analysis. OPR formulates long-range state goals and policies to address four key areas: land use  

[8:1:66,330] and planning, climate risk and resilience, sustainable economic development, and targeted long-range research needs. OPR  

[8:2:66,340] acts as the state's liaison to a variety of entities including local and regional government, planning professionals, small  

[8:3:66,350] business, and the Department of Defense. OPR also serves a role as convener across multiple agencies on cross-cutting  

[8:4:66,360] issues in the four key areas of our work.  

[9:0:66,381] The Office of Planning and Research includes three main budget programs: the State Policy Program (often referred to as  

[9:1:66,391] OPR), which houses most of core planning and research functions; the California Strategic Growth Council, which coordinates  

[9:2:66,401] and works collaboratively with public agencies, communities, and stakeholders to realize healthy, thriving, resilient communities  

[9:3:66,411] for all and provides direct grant funding to projects demonstrating the value and feasibility of sustainable communities; and  

[9:4:66,421] California Volunteers, a program aimed to increase the number and impact of Californians involved with service and  

[9:5:66,432] volunteering throughout the state.

[4:0:66,177] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[5:0:65,196] • Youth Workforce Development —The Budget includes $185 million one-time   federal American Rescue Plan Act of 2021  

[5:1:76,207] (ARPA)   funds to administer grants to cities and counties to create or expand youth employment opportunities.  

[6:0:65,223] • CaliforniansForAll College Service   Program—The Budget includes $146.3 million one-time ($18.8 million General Fund and  

[6:1:76,233] $127.5 million ARPA) to provide paid service opportunities for college students in critical issue areas such as climate action,  

[6:2:76,243] education and youth development, tutoring and mentoring, health, and disaster response.  

[7:0:65,259] • Student Success Coach Grant Program—The Budget includes $15 million one-time General Fund to establish student  

[7:1:76,270] success coaches in communities to support K-12 students.  

[8:0:65,286] • Precision Medicine—The Budget includes $12.4 million one-time General Fund for seven projects focused on advancing  

[8:1:76,296] research on, and building scalable approaches to, treating and preventing adverse childhood experiences.  

[9:0:65,312] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package: Innovative Wood Products — The Budget includes $3 million one-time General Fund  

[9:1:76,322] in 2020-21 as a part of a comprehensive package to increase the pace and scale of forest health activities and reduce  

[9:2:76,333] wildfire risk.  

[10:0:65,349] • California Volunteers Statewide Emergency Response Framework—The Budget includes $1.3 million ongoing General Fund  

[10:1:76,359] to maintain an emergency response framework capable of preparing, training, and mobilizing a collective team of disaster  

[10:2:76,369] volunteers efficiently and effectively.  

[11:0:65,385] • Regional Climate Collaborative Program Implementation—The Budget includes $508,000 ongoing General Fund to continue  

[11:1:76,396] work already underway to fulfill the requirements of Chapter 377, Statues of 2018 (SB 1072).

[9:0:66,417] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[10:0:66,436] 0360 - STATE PLANNING AND POLICY DEVELOPMENT  

[11:0:66,454] By statute, the office is the state comprehensive planning agency. The major activities of the State Planning and Policy  

[11:1:66,464] Development program can be grouped into four major categories: land use and planning, climate risk and resilience,  

[11:2:66,474] sustainable economic development, and targeted long-range research needs. More specifically these include: (1)  

[11:3:66,485] recommending and implementing state policies on land-use and growth planning and compliance with environmental statutes  

[11:4:66,495] and regulations, including General Plan Guidelines and other guidance to local and regional governments; (2) coordinating the  

[11:5:66,505] implementation of the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), including: (a) operating the State Clearinghouse; (b)  

[11:6:66,515] maintaining an online database for the electronic submission and archiving of CEQA documents; (c) developing CEQA  

[11:7:66,525] guidelines amendments; (d) providing technical assistance; (e) providing training, and (f) coordinating AB 900 project review (3)  

[11:8:66,536] supporting the Governor's Advisor for Military Affairs and Governor's Military Council; (4) providing policy research for the  

[11:9:66,546] Governor and Cabinet; (5) supporting local planning efforts related to the impacts of droughts and wildfires;(6) supporting  

[11:10:66,556] development of state plans including, but not limited to, the Water Action Plan, the State Multi-Hazard Mitigation Plan, the  

[11:11:66,566] Safeguarding California Plan, the Integrated Energy Policy Report, the 5-Year Infrastructure Plan, and the 5th Climate  

[11:12:66,576] Assessment; (7) supporting multiple efforts to address climate change and response to carbon pollution; (8) administering the  

[11:13:66,587] Integrated Climate Adaptation and Resiliency Program to coordinate regional and local efforts with state climate adaptation  

[11:14:66,597] strategies to adapt to the impacts of climate change; (9) housing the Federal Grants Administrator for tracking of federal grants  

[11:15:66,607] coming to California and providing information on federal grants to help maximize federal grant opportunities within the state;  

[11:16:66,617] (10) administering the California Education Learning Lab, California Initiative to Advance Precision Medicine, the Cradle-to-

[11:17:66,627] Career, and Higher Education Innovation grant programs; (11) Establishing and facilitating the Rural Economic Development  

[11:18:66,638] Steering Committee/Wood Utilization Work Group to promote rural economic development that drives healthy and sustainable  

[11:19:66,648] forest management consistent with our rural economic development, public health and safety, and climate goals; and (13)  

[11:20:66,658] support the state’s effort on a “just transition” to a carbon neutral economy by 2045 (14) conducting other activities as the  

[11:21:66,668] Governor may direct.  

[12:0:66,686] 0365 - CALIFORNIA VOLUNTEERS  

[13:0:66,704] California Volunteers is the state office tasked with engaging Californians in services, volunteering and civic action to tackle our  

[13:1:66,714] state’s most pressing challenges while lifting up all communities. From the California Climate Action Corps, the country’s first

[3:0:66,69] statewide climate corps program, to the #CaliforniansForAll service initiative launched in response to COVID-19 to establish a  

[3:1:66,79] volunteer corps to support the state’s response to emergencies and disasters, California Volunteers plays an integral role in  

[3:2:66,89] tackling the state’s problems. California Volunteers is led by the State’s Chief Service Officer and supported by a bipartisan  

[3:3:66,99] 25-member Commission and a team of experts driven by a mission to empower and mobilize all Californians to volunteer and  

[3:4:66,109] serve in their communities.  

[4:0:66,127] 0370 - CALIFORNIA STRATEGIC GROWTH COUNCIL  

[5:0:66,145] The California Strategic Growth Council (SGC) coordinates and works collaboratively with public agencies, communities, and  

[5:1:66,155] stakeholders to advance healthy, thriving, and resilient communities for all. SGC integrates State, regional, and local efforts to  

[5:2:66,165] reduce greenhouse gas emissions; increase the availability of affordable housing located near jobs, services, and green space;  

[5:3:66,175] conserve agriculture lands; promote public health; and advance racial equity. SGC administers the following grant programs:  

[5:4:66,186] Affordable Housing and Sustainable Communities; Transformative Climate Communities; Sustainable Agricultural Lands  

[5:5:66,196] Conservation; Climate Change Research – all of which are part of the California Climate Investments Program, funded by  

[5:6:66,206] proceeds from the State’s cap-and-trade auction revenues. SGC operates these funding programs in collaboration with  

[5:7:66,216] interagency teams to develop program guidelines, review applications, and administer program funds. SGC’s Community  

[5:8:66,226] Assistance for Climate Equity Program encompasses both the Regional Climate Collaboratives, which supports local capacity  

[5:9:66,237] building and technical assistance in under-resourced communities across California; and the California Climate Investments  

[5:10:66,247] Technical Assistance Program, which increases access to SGC’s own grant programs as well as those of many other State  

[5:11:66,257] agencies. SGC partners with High-Speed Rail Authority (HSRA) to support sustainable development and conservation  

[5:12:66,267] practices, especially in future HSR station areas. SGC’s Health and Equity Program advances the integration of health and  

[5:13:66,277] equity principles in State programs and planning through the Health in All Policies Task Force, partnerships and collaborations,  

[5:14:66,288] and in the implementation of SGC’s Racial Equity Action Plan. Finally, SGC is charged with reviewing the State’s 5-Year  

[5:15:66,298] Infrastructure Plan and to contribute to interagency efforts to promote sustainability, equity, and resilience in State investments.

[11:0:202,548] 0690   Office of Emergency Services  

[12:0:66,570] The principal objective of the Office of Emergency Services (Cal OES) is to protect lives and property, build emergency  

[12:1:66,580] response capabilities, and support communities for a resilient California. The Cal OES Director serves as both the Governor's  

[12:2:66,590] Homeland Security Advisor and Emergency Management Director, with responsibility to implement and facilitate the state's  

[12:3:66,600] homeland security and counter-terrorism strategy. Cal OES serves the public through effective collaboration in preparing for,  

[12:4:66,611] protecting against, responding to, recovering from, and mitigating the impacts of hazards and threats.  

[12:5:66,628] Cal OES provides leadership, assistance, training, and support to state and local agencies and coordinates with federal  

[12:6:66,638] agencies in planning and preparing for the most effective use of federal, state, local, and private sector resources in  

[12:7:66,649] emergencies. This emergency planning is based upon a system of mutual aid whereby a jurisdiction relies first on its own  

[12:8:66,659] resources, and then requests assistance from its neighbors. Cal OES' plans and programs are coordinated with those of the  

[12:9:66,669] federal government, other states, private sector, utilities, and state and local agencies within California.  

[12:10:66,687] During an emergency, Cal OES functions as the Governor's immediate staff to provide guidance and coordinate the state's  

[12:11:66,697] responsibilities under the Emergency Services Act and applicable federal statutes. It also acts as the conduit for federal  

[12:12:66,707] assistance through natural disaster grants and federal agency support. Additionally, Cal OES is responsible for the  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] development and coordination of a comprehensive state strategy related to all hazards, including terrorism, which includes  

[3:1:66,79] prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery.  

[4:0:66,97] Cal OES implements the state's homeland security strategy by overseeing the California Cybersecurity Integration Center and  

[4:1:66,107] the State Threat Assessment System (STAS), which includes management of the State Threat Assessment Center. The STAS  

[4:2:66,117] assists in the detection, prevention, and investigation of   and response to criminal and terrorist activity and   also facilitates  

[4:3:66,127] information and intelligence sharing between local, state, federal, tribal agencies, and private sector partners.  

[5:0:66,145] The Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety   Commission   is a separate unit within the   Office of Emergency Services. Its mission is to  

[5:1:66,155] lower earthquake risk to life and property of Californians. The Commission works with federal, state, and local agencies, as well  

[5:2:66,165] as the private sector, on a variety of activities that guide and stimulate earthquake risk reduction and management.  

[5:3:66,183] Because Cal OES' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Cal OES has a capital outlay program to support this  

[5:4:66,193] need. For the specifics on Cal OES' capital outlay program, see "Infrastructure Overview."

[3:0:66,88] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[4:0:65,107] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package: Home Hardening and Community Protection—Chapter 14, Statutes of 2021 (SB 85)  

[4:1:76,118] provided $21.9 million one-time General Fund in 2020-21 as a part of a comprehensive package to increase the pace and  

[4:2:76,128] scale of forest health activities and reduce wildfire risk. In total, the Budget includes a $1.5 billion wildfire prevention and  

[4:3:76,138] forest resilience investment over three years ($536 million in 2020 21, $458 million in 2021-22, and $500 million in 2022 23).  

[4:4:76,148] Allocations for the 2021-22 and 2022-23 budgets will be determined in subsequent legislation.  

[5:0:65,164] • Building Resilience—The Budget includes $100 million one-time General Fund for   Community Hardening to Build Disaster  

[5:1:76,175] Resilient Communities. Specifically, this funding will support community hardening efforts within under-resourced  

[5:2:76,185] communities by covering the local government’s cost share for participating in the federal hazard mitigation grant program  

[5:3:76,195] and providing outreach and technical assistance to disadvantaged local communities and governments as they apply for  

[5:4:76,205] federal grants.  

[6:0:65,221] • Victims of Crime Act Supplemental Funding—The Budget includes $100 million one-time General Fund to supplement  

[6:1:76,232] federal funding supporting a variety of services for domestic violence victims. This one-time augmentation will allow existing  

[6:2:76,242] programs to continue, while building capacity to handle the increased need.  

[7:0:65,258] • Strengthening California’s Emergency Response Capacity and Capabilities—The Budget includes $60.6 million ongoing  

[7:1:76,268] General Fund and 114 positions to strengthen California’s emergency capacity and capabilities to respond to increasingly  

[7:2:76,278] complex disasters, security threats, and emergencies; maximize federal funds for disasters; modernize technology and data  

[7:3:76,289] analytics; and strengthen equity and connections with vulnerable populations. Specifically, the budget includes the following:  

[8:0:74,306] ◦ Incident Response Team—$16.5 million ongoing General Fund and 73 positions to establish permanent and dedicated  

[8:1:85,316] incident response teams and needed support to operate California’s State Operations Center or Regional Emergency  

[8:2:85,327] Operations Centers to respond to all-hazard events. These teams will strengthen the capabilities of the State Operations  

[8:3:85,337] Center and lessen the Department’s reliance on redirected staff from other areas in the organization.  

[9:0:74,353] ◦ Strengthening Equity and Connections with Communities—$29.9 million ongoing General Fund and 13 positions to  

[9:1:85,363] establish a new Office of Equity and make permanent an annual $25 million Listos California grant program to further  

[9:2:85,373] strengthen connections and build resiliency with all communities.  

[10:0:74,390] ◦ Modernizing Technology and Data Analytics—$10.2 million ongoing General Fund and 14 positions to modernize the  

[10:1:85,400] Department’s technology and data capabilities through new technology and updating outdated systems.  

[11:0:74,416] ◦ Strengthening Tracking and Reporting of Disaster Costs and Funding—$4 million ongoing General Fund and 14 positions  

[11:1:85,426] to establish a new unit within Cal OES to better coordinate and lead the state’s disaster cost tracking and reporting efforts  

[11:2:85,436] in coordination with all state agencies. This new unit will serve as a liaison. 

[12:0:65,460] • Nonprofit Security Grant Program—The Budget includes $50 million one-time General Fund for the California State Nonprofit  

[12:1:76,470] Security Grant Program, to assist nonprofit organizations that have historically been targets of hate-motivated violence. This  

[12:2:76,481] program, administered by the Cal OES, provides support for target hardening and other physical security enhancements to  

[12:3:76,491] nonprofit organizations that are at high risk of violence and hate crimes based on ideology and beliefs.  

[13:0:65,507] • Long Term Recovery Support—The Budget includes $37.8 million ($15.7 million General Fund) ongoing to provide  

[13:1:76,517] permanent funding for 104 permanent recovery positions provided in 2018 with three-year limited-term funding, and 110 new  

[13:2:76,527] positions to better support California’s recovery efforts and recoupment of eligible federal disaster cost reimbursements for  

[13:3:76,538] the state and local governments, and to maximize the hazard mitigation program to mitigate the impacts of future disasters  

[13:4:76,548] on the state and local jurisdictions.  

[14:0:65,564] • COVID-19 Direct Response Expenditures—The Budget includes a total of $1.7 billion for various state departments for direct  

[14:1:76,574] response costs associated with responding to and mitigating the impacts of the COVID-19 Pandemic in 2021-22.  

[14:2:76,584] Specifically, the budget includes   $18.9 million one-time General Fund for Cal OES for state response operation costs to  

[14:3:76,595] continue providing resources through the State Operation Center and funding subject matter experts for various response  

[14:4:76,605] activities.  

[15:0:65,621] • California Earthquake Early Warning System—The Budget includes $17.3 million one-time General Fund to continue to  

[15:1:76,631] support system operations and program management, an education and outreach campaign, and research and development  

[15:2:76,641] to expand earthquake mitigation use of the system.  

[16:0:65,658] • Gun Violence Restraining Orders—The Budget includes $11 million one-time General Fund to facilitate education and  

[16:1:76,668] training efforts related to gun violence restraining orders. This funding includes: (1) $5 million for to conduct a public  

[16:2:76,678] awareness campaign, (2) $5 million for grants to domestic violence groups to conduct outreach, and (3) $1 million to the City  

[16:3:76,688] of San Diego for the city attorney to provide temporary gun violence restraining order trainings to entities statewide.  

[17:0:65,704] • Medical Evidentiary Sexual Assault Examination Reimbursement Claims—The Budget includes $6.7 million ongoing General  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[4:3:65,142] • Wildfire Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center (SB 209)—The Budget includes $7.5 million General Fund in  

[4:4:76,152] 2021-22, $4.5 million ongoing General Fund, and 22 positions for Cal OES, California Military Department, Department of  

[4:5:76,162] Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE), and Public Utilities Commission to implement Chapter 405, Statutes of 2019 (SB  

[4:6:76,172] 209) which requires Cal OES and CAL FIRE to establish a Wildfire Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center  

[4:7:76,183] (Wildfire Forecast Center). The Wildfire Forecast Center will: (1) provide “real time” intelligence and data regarding wildfire  

[4:8:76,193] threats to government agencies; (2) develop intelligence products for public and private entities involved in wildfire risk  

[4:9:76,203] mitigation efforts; and (3) serve as a central organizing hub for wildfire forecasting, weather information, threat intelligence  

[4:10:76,213] gathering, and analysis. This additional investment builds on the $2 million ongoing General Fund provided in the 2020  

[4:11:76,223] Budget Act for a total investment of $9.5 million, of which $2.8 million is appropriated to Cal OES.

[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — LJE   128 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:167,32] 0690   Office of Emergency Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,66] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[4:0:66,85] 0380 - EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT SERVICES  

[5:0:66,105] Working through the state's mutual aid system, the Emergency Management Services and Homeland Security Program  

[5:1:66,115] coordinates the provision   of federal, state, and local resources to jurisdictions whose resources and services are overextended  

[5:2:66,126] in a disaster situation. In addition, this program develops and coordinates a comprehensive state strategy related to an all-

[5:3:66,136] hazards approach that includes prevention, preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery consistent with the National  

[5:4:66,146] Strategy on Homeland Security. Central to this effort is maintaining operational readiness at all levels of government. Within this  

[5:5:66,156] program, Cal OES also trains emergency managers, responders, and state and local leaders in preparedness and response to  

[5:6:66,166] all hazards.  

[6:0:66,184] 0385 - SPECIAL PROGRAMS AND GRANT MANAGEMENT  

[7:0:66,202] Cal OES serves as the State Administering Agent for federal homeland security, emergency management, and victim services  

[7:1:66,212] grants and is the primary liaison with the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the United States Department of Homeland  

[7:2:66,222] Security, and other federal agencies. Additionally, Cal OES serves as an advocate for local public safety, emergency  

[7:3:66,232] management, and public and private agencies operating public safety and victim service programs. As part of its grant  

[7:4:66,243] management role, Cal OES administers the California Disaster Assistance Act and provides eligible local agencies with  

[7:5:66,253] financial assistance to recover from disasters. Included in this program is the State Threat Assessment Center, which is the  

[7:6:66,263] centerpiece of the state's intelligence information sharing environment and serves as the Governor's designated primary fusion  

[7:7:66,273] center.  

[8:0:66,291] 0390 - ALFRED E. ALQUIST SEISMIC SAFETY COMMISSION  

[9:0:66,309] The Alfred E. Alquist Seismic Safety Commission program provides a coordinated framework for establishing consistent  

[9:1:66,319] earthquake policies; advising the Governor, the Legislature, local governments, and the public; and tracking the state’s  

[9:2:66,329] progress toward higher levels of seismic safety.   The Commission uses the expertise of its commissioners to review, evaluate,  

[9:3:66,339] and translate scientific information and make recommendations to guide and influence earthquake safety policies.   The  

[9:4:66,349] Commission works with federal, state, and local agencies, as well as the private sector, on a variety of activities that support  

[9:5:66,360] the state’s earthquake preparedness, mitigation, response, and recovery efforts.   These activities include: (1) developing and  

[9:6:66,370] reviewing seismic safety projects; (2) providing consistent policy direction; (3) proposing and reviewing earthquake-related  

[9:7:66,380] legislation; (4) conducting public hearings on seismic safety issues; (5) using existing knowledge and conducting studies,  

[9:8:66,390] where necessary, to improve the performance of structures in California, (6) recommending earthquake safety programs and  

[9:9:66,400] supporting cost-effective partnerships that help reduce earthquake risks and speed economic recovery.  

[10:0:66,418] 0395 - PUBLIC SAFETY COMMUNICATIONS  

[11:0:66,439] The Public Safety Communications   program is responsible for the design, installation, maintenance, and repair of the  

[11:1:66,449] state's   public safety communications networks and oversight of the state's 9-1-1 system. The   program is dedicated to the  

[11:2:66,459] preservation and protection of human life and public safety by delivering reliable and dependable communication services that  

[11:3:66,469] keep the public connected during times of crisis.  

[12:0:66,487] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION AND EXECUTIVE  

[13:0:66,507] The Administration and Executive Program provides the overall policy direction of   Cal OES and support services such as  

[13:1:66,517] accounting, fiscal, personnel, information technology, and business services.  

[14:0:66,558] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[8:0:66,461] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:0:66,480] The Office of Emergency Services' infrastructure includes a headquarters facility, an administrative building, and an Inland  

[9:1:66,490] Regional Operations Center in Sacramento County; a Coastal Regional Operations Center in Fairfield in Solano   County; a  

[9:2:66,501] Southern Regional Operations Center located at Los Alamitos Joint Forces Training Base in Orange County; the California  

[9:3:66,511] Specialized Training Institute at Camp San Luis Obispo; and various small field offices throughout the state.  

[10:0:66,531] The Public Safety Communications division (PSC) is comprised of a main leased complex in Sacramento and 44 field locations  

[10:1:66,541] throughout the state. These locations include 7 area offices and 37 area shops, positioned geographically to facilitate  

[10:2:66,552] maintenance and installation services to remote communication sites and customers throughout the state. In addition, the PSC  

[10:3:66,562] maintains and operates 11 communications vaults/towers and a total of more than 3,500 radio frequency points of presence.
