[12:0:66,586] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[13:0:66,605] 0860 - POLLUTION CONTROL TAX-EXEMPT BOND PROGRAM  

[14:0:66,626] The objective of this program is to provide bond financing to California businesses, irrespective of company size, for the  

[14:1:66,636] acquisition, construction, or installation of qualified pollution control, waste disposal, water furnishing, wastewater treatment,  

[14:2:66,646] waste recovery facilities, and the acquisition and installation of new equipment. Tax-exempt financing issued by the Authority  

[14:3:66,656] assists municipalities in complying with waste diversion mandates of the California Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989,  

[14:4:66,667] the California Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006, and more recently, Assembly Bill 1826 (Chesbro, Chapter 727, Statutes of  

[14:5:66,677] 2014), and Senate Bill 1383 (Lara, Chapter 395, Statutes of 2016). As of June 30, 2020, bonds totaling approximately $16.4  

[14:6:66,687] billion have been issued by the Authority.  

[15:0:66,705] 0865 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR SMALL BUSINESSES  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 241 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — LJE   239 

[2:0:116,32] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The objective of this program is to   inspire banks and other financial institutions to make loans to small businesses. CalCAP for  

[3:1:66,79] Small Business provides a form of loan loss recovery with up to 100 percent coverage on certain loan defaults. By participating  

[3:2:66,89] in CalCAP, lenders have   available to them a   proven financing mechanism to assist the financing needs of California's small  

[3:3:66,99] businesses. The program works through   the creation of   a loan loss-reserve fund specific to each   participating financial  

[3:4:66,109] institution. The Authority, financial institution, and borrower all contribute to fund the reserve. The Authority provides  

[4:0:66,120] additional   incentives for   lending to businesses located in underserved communities and disaster areas. The reserve serves to  

[4:1:66,130] reduce loan-loss risk, encouraging   banks to lend to targeted California small businesses.  

[5:0:66,148] Prior to 2010, CalCAP was entirely self-funded with $38 million transferred from CPCFA's Small Business Assistance Fund  

[5:1:66,158] (SBAF) established   with fees collected from large companies financed through CPCFA's   tax-exempt bond program.   In October  

[5:2:66,168] 2010, Chapter 731, Statutes of 2010, appropriated $6 million from the General Fund to CalCAP to increase lending efforts.   The  

[5:3:66,178] $6 million appropriation   was fully-expended by the end of 2018 allowing the annual recapture of contributions to become the  

[5:4:66,188] primary source for the State's contributions.  

[6:0:66,209] In   2011 California was awarded an allocation of federal funds in the amount of $168 million from the federal State Small  

[6:1:66,219] Business Credit Initiative (SSBCI). The allocation was shared between the Authority, $85 million, and   the Governor's Office of  

[6:2:66,229] Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz), $83   million.   These funds enabled the Authority to expand the availability of its  

[6:3:66,239] CalCAP for Small Business   statewide.   The Authority also launched the   Collateral Support Program   to   offset lenders resistance  

[6:4:66,250] to lend after the 2008 recession.   The federal funds   were expended by the fourth quarter of 2018, allowing for all recaptured  

[6:5:66,260] funds to stay with its state.   Funds are being   recaptured annually to sustain both the State CalCAP for Small Business and the  

[6:6:66,270] Collateral Support Program.  

[7:0:66,290] Utilizing the CalCAP model, the Authority   partnered with the California Air Resources Board (ARB) to assist   truck and bus  

[7:1:66,301] owners in meeting new clean air requirements by directing a total of $215.9 million in Air Quality Improvement Program funds  

[7:2:66,311] to incentivize lending for cleaner vehicles that   comply with ARB's Statewide Truck and Bus Rule. The Authority   also partnered  

[7:3:66,321] with the California Energy Commission to implement a $2 million pilot program to provide credit enhancements and rebates to  

[7:4:66,331] small business borrowers and multi-unit dwellings to   install electric vehicle charging stations.  

[8:0:66,349] 0870 - CALIFORNIA RECYCLE UNDERUTILIZED SITES PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,369] The CALReUSE program assists with the reuse and redevelopment of underutilized properties with real or perceived  

[9:1:66,379] contamination issues. CALReUSE addresses a funding and information gap in the development of contaminated properties  

[9:2:66,390] (brownfields) to help bring these properties into productive reuse. Beginning in 2000, the CALReUSE Assessment Program  

[9:3:66,400] provided forgivable loans of up to $300,000 which was later increased to $500,000 per brownfield site. The funds assisted with  

[9:4:66,410] brownfield site assessment and characterization, technical assistance, and the development of remedial action plans. Eligible  

[9:5:66,420] projects included sites with potential beneficial reuse not currently redeveloped due to lack of information about real or  

[9:6:66,430] perceived contamination, uncertainty about clean-up costs, or concerns regarding time frames and the regulatory process.  

[10:0:66,451] Additionally, Proposition 1C, the Housing and Emergency Shelter Trust Fund Act of 2006, included provisions to fund  

[11:0:66,461] brownfield cleanup that resulted in the expansion of the CALReUSE Program. The CALReUSE Remediation Program received  

[11:1:66,471] $60 million in Proposition 1C funds and provided loans and grants up to $5 million per project   for   brownfield remediation that  

[11:2:66,481] promoted affordable infill residential and mixed-used developments. In one instance, CALReUSE provided a grant in excess of  

[11:3:66,492] $5 million in order to utilize returned funds.   All CALReUSE Program funds have been awarded. The CALReUSE Program's  

[11:4:66,502] $60 million investment in cleanup of contaminated sites will result in 5,587 housing units, of which more than 47% will be  

[11:5:66,512] affordable, in 30 California communities.  

[12:0:66,530] 0875 - ALTERNATIVE FINANCING PROGRAMS  

[13:0:66,547] Under its authority to provide alternative methods of financing facilities for pollution control, clean water supply and alternative  

[13:1:66,558] energy production, the Authority is developing alternative financing models to assist sister agencies with achieving the state's  

[13:2:66,568] environmental goals. The Authority is authorized to receive and accept contributions from other sources including federal and  

[13:3:66,578] state agencies to carry out the purposes of its statutory authority.  

[14:0:66,596] 0876 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR COMPLIANCE WITH THE AMERICANS WITH DISABLITIES ACT  

[15:0:66,616] The objective of this program, created by Chapter 787, Statutes of 2015, is to help small businesses comply with the federal  

[15:1:66,626] Americans with Disabilities Act and increase access for   employees and customers by encouraging lenders and other financial  

[15:2:66,637] institutions to make loans to very small businesses to finance tenant improvements. The Legislature provided the Authority an  

[15:3:66,647] appropriation of $10 million effective January 1, 2016, to administer a credit enhancement program following the CalCAP  

[15:4:66,657] model   incentivizing lenders to enroll qualifying loans.   The program also offers borrowers a rebate toward the cost of the  

[15:5:66,667] Certified Access Specialist (CASp) Report.  

[16:0:66,685] 0877 - CALIFORNIA SEISMIC SAFETY CAPITAL ACCESS LOAN PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,705] The California Seismic Safety Capital Access Loan Program, authorized by Chapter 32, Statutes of 2016, provided a one-time  

[17:1:66,715] allocation of $10 million effective January 1, 2017 to administer a credit enhancement   program for   qualified small businesses

[8:0:66,460] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,479] 0880 - CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,497] In 2004, California voters approved Proposition 61, which established the Children's Hospital Program. In 2008, Proposition 3  

[9:1:66,507] established the second Children's Hospital Program. In 2018, Proposition 4 established a third Children’s Hospital Program.  

[9:2:66,517] The purpose of the three programs is to improve the health and welfare of California's critically ill children by providing a stable  

[9:3:66,527] and ready source of funds for capital improvement projects for children's hospitals and, under Proposition 4, hospitals that  

[9:4:66,538] provide pediatric services for children eligible for California Children’s Services. Children's Hospital Program grant funding is  

[9:5:66,548] derived from the issuance of general obligation bonds, up to $750 million under Proposition 61, $980 million under Proposition  

[9:6:66,558] 3, and $1.5 billion under Proposition 4.  

[10:0:66,576] 0885 - HEALTH FACILITIES BONDS, LOANS, AND GRANTS  

[11:0:66,596] The Authority serves as a conduit provider of tax-exempt bonds and a direct provider of financial assistance to health  

[11:1:66,606] institutions.  

[12:0:66,627] Tax-Exempt Bond Program  

[13:0:66,647] Bonds issued under this conduit program are not a debt, liability, or a pledge of the full faith and credit of the taxing power of  

[13:1:66,657] the state or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating institutions are pledged for repayment of  

[13:2:66,667] the bonds. To qualify for funding, the proposed project must be a health facility, operated by a private nonprofit corporation or  

[13:3:66,678] association, city, city and county, county, or hospital district.  

[14:0:66,698] HELP II Loan Program  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:119,32] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The Authority administers the Healthcare Expansion Loan Program II (HELP II) to assist small and rural health facilities in  

[3:1:66,79] obtaining financing for their capital needs. The HELP II Program provides two percent and three percent fixed interest rate  

[3:2:66,89] loans of up to $1.5 million to California's nonprofit small and rural health facilities in an efficient, timely, and cost effective  

[3:3:66,99] manner. HELP II loans may be used to purchase or construct new facilities, remodel or renovate existing facilities, purchase  

[3:4:66,109] equipment and/or furnishings, and refinance existing debt.  

[3:5:66,127] 0890 - MENTAL HEALTH WELLNESS GRANTS  

[4:0:66,145] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 Grant Program 

[5:0:66,163] Chapter 34, Statutes of 2013 (SB 82), the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 and related legislation provided  

[5:1:66,173] $142.5 million in one-time General Fund, and $4 million in ongoing Mental Health Services Act funding to provide grants for  

[5:2:66,183] community based mental health crisis support.  

[6:0:66,201] The Act established a grant program to disburse funds to California counties or to their nonprofit or public agency designees for  

[6:1:66,211] the purpose of developing mental health crisis support programs. Specifically, funds may be used to increase capacity for client  

[6:2:66,221] assistance and services in crisis intervention, crisis stabilization, crisis residential treatment, rehabilitative mental health  

[6:3:66,231] services, and mobile crisis support teams. The grants will support capital improvement, expansion and limited start-up costs.  

[7:0:66,252] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for Children and Youth  

[8:0:66,269] Chapter 30, Statutes of 2016 (SB 833), Section 20 created the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for  

[8:1:66,280] Children and Youth to increase mental health services for children and youth 21 years of age and under to develop a complete  

[8:2:66,290] continuum of crisis services. Working with counties, the program funds facility acquisition, construction/renovation, equipment  

[8:3:66,300] acquisition, applicable startup or expansion costs, and limited personnel funding to develop crisis residential treatment and  

[8:4:66,310] crisis stabilization facilities, mobile crisis support teams, and family respite care facilities.  

[9:0:66,331] • Community Services Infrastructure Grants 

[10:0:66,348] Chapter 33, Statutes of 2016 (SB 843), Section 52 created the Community Services Infrastructure competitive grant program to  

[10:1:66,358] expand community alternatives to jail and prison. The program seeks to expand access to jail and prison diversion programs  

[10:2:66,369] and services for those with mental health illness, substance use disorders, or who have suffered from trauma. Working with  

[10:3:66,379] counties, the program funds facility acquisition, renovation, equipment acquisition, and applicable startup or expansion costs for  

[10:4:66,389] facilities that provide services to this population.  

[10:5:66,407] • No Place Like Home Program  

[11:0:66,424] Chapter 43, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1618) established the No Place Like Home Program (NPLH), which requires the Department  

[11:1:66,435] of Housing and Community Development (HCD) to award up to $2 billion to counties for the acquisition, design, construction,  

[11:2:66,445] rehabilitation, or preservation of permanent supportive housing for individuals living with a severe mental illness who are  

[11:3:66,455] homeless or at risk of chronic homelessness. Chapter 322, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1628) authorized CHFFA to enter into  

[11:4:66,465] contracts with HCD and further authorized CHFFA to issue up to $2 billion in taxable revenue bonds pursuant to the NPLH  

[11:5:66,475] Program. Chapter 41, Statutes of 2018 (AB 1827) placed the No Place Like Home program on the November 2018 ballot  

[11:6:66,486] (Proposition 2), where it was adopted by the voters as the No Place Like Home Act. This ratified existing law establishing the  

[11:7:66,496] No Place Like Home program as being consistent with the Mental Health Services Act approved through Proposition 63 in  

[11:8:66,506] 2004. It also ratified the issuance of up to $2 billion in previously authorized bonds.

[13:0:211,554] 0981   California ABLE Act Board  

[14:0:66,575] The California Achieving a Better Life Experience Act (ABLE) Board was established by Chapter 796, Statutes of 2015 (SB  

[14:1:66,585] 324) to create a Qualified ABLE Program. The Program will provide eligible individuals with disabilities the opportunity to save  

[14:2:66,596] private funds in tax-advantaged ABLE accounts for the purpose of supporting persons with disabilities to maintain their health,  

[14:3:66,606] independence, and quality of life.  

[15:0:66,626] The Board consists of the State Treasurer (Chair), the Director of Finance, the Controller, the Director of Developmental  

[15:1:66,636] Services, the Chairperson of the State Council on Developmental Disabilities, the Director of Rehabilitation, and the Chair of  

[15:2:66,647] the State Independent Living Council.

[11:0:66,522] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,541] 0895 - CALIFORNIA ABLE ACT BOARD  

[12:0:66,561] On December 19, 2014, President Obama signed the Stephen Beck Jr., Achieving a Better Life Experience Act of 2014, which  

[12:1:66,571] allows individuals who became blind or disabled before reaching age 26, to create tax-free savings accounts. Individuals can  

[12:2:66,582] make nondeductible cash contributions to an ABLE account in the name of a designated beneficiary with tax-free earnings.  

[12:3:66,592] ABLE account distributions are also not included in the beneficiary's income, as long as they are used for qualified disability  

[12:4:66,602] expenses. Chapter 774, Statutes of 2015 (AB 449), implements the ABLE Act in California, and directs the Board, with the  

[12:5:66,612] State Treasurer as Chair, to administer the California ABLE Program accounts on behalf of eligible individuals.  

[13:0:66,633] The Board and the California ABLE Program Trust were established by Chapter 796, Statutes of 2015 (SB 324), for the  

[13:1:66,643] purpose of creating the Program. CalABLE was officially launched in December of 2018 with the mission to meet the diverse  

[13:2:66,653] needs of our customers and their families. CalABLE’s pledge is to be customer-driven, accountable, and a trusted partner in  

[13:3:66,663] providing financial services.

[7:0:66,274] CSFA serves as the primary state agency for California charter schools seeking facility and working capital assistance by  

[7:1:66,284] providing access to low-cost financing through several State and federally funded programs.

[6:0:66,393] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,413] 0918 - SMART BONDS PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,433] Chapter 1438, Statutes of 1985, as amended by Chapter 598, Statutes of 1987, established CSFA and authorized the issuance  

[7:1:66,443] of revenue bonds or other debt instruments. The proceeds from the sale of the bonds were available for loans to schools and  

[7:2:66,453] community college districts to assist with the acquisition of equipment and new school sites, construction of new facilities,  

[7:3:66,464] reconstruction of existing facilities, capital improvements, and to provide working capital. Chapter 325, Statutes of 2006 (AB  

[7:4:66,474] 2717), allows CSFA to issue debt on behalf of California charter schools for capital improvements and working capital needs.  

[7:5:66,491] 0920 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,512] Chapter 935, Statutes of 2002, as amended by Chapter 587, Statutes of 2003, created the Charter School Facilities Program  

[8:1:66,522] (CSFP), which provides funding for the new construction, renovation or rehabilitation of charter school facilities. CSFA and the  

[8:2:66,532] Office of Public School Construction jointly administer the program. CSFP was funded through the Kindergarten-University  

[8:3:66,542] Public Education Facilities Bond Acts of 2002, 2004, 2006, and 2016, for a total of $1.4 billion. The program provides a 50  

[8:4:66,553] percent state subsidy for the charter school facilities project costs, with the balance of the project costs being repaid (to the  

[8:5:66,563] state) by the charter school in the form of a long-term lease. Recipient charter schools must provide site-based instruction, be  

[8:6:66,573] deemed financially sound by CSFA, and meet other program eligibility requirements.  

[8:7:66,591] 0925 - STATE CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITIES INCENTIVE GRANTS PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,611] In 2004, 2009, and 2014, CSFA was awarded grants under the United States Department of Education's State Charter School  

[9:1:66,621] Facilities Incentive Grants Program (CFDA 84.282D) to provide California charter schools with assistance for facilities costs.  

[9:2:66,632] Grant funds reimburse a charter school's costs for rent, lease, mortgage or debt service payments for existing or new facilities  

[9:3:66,642] or the costs of acquiring land and/or constructing or renovating a facility. Grants are awarded to charter schools based on  

[9:4:66,652] preference points allotted by the percentage of low-income students, percentage of overcrowding, not-for-profit status, and  

[9:5:66,662] demonstrated student performance. Schools cannot use program funds to supplant state funding.  

[9:6:66,680] 0930 - CHARTER SCHOOL FACILITY GRANT PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,700] The Charter School Facility Grant Program provides Proposition 98 General Fund grants to assist charter schools with rent and  

[10:1:66,710] lease costs. This program targets schools and communities with significant populations of economically disadvantaged  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:2:66,97] 0935 - CHARTER SCHOOL REVOLVING LOAN FUND  

[4:0:66,117] Charter 1010, Statutes of 1976 established the Charter School Revolving Loan Fund in the State Treasury to provide loans of

[4:2:66,137] available to any charter school that is not a conversion of an existing public school, and preference is given to schools that  

[4:3:66,148] have not opened nor   completed their full charter term.

[7:0:168,306] 0989   California Educational Facilities Authority  

[7:1:66,328] The California Educational Facilities Authority (CEFA) provides qualified nonprofit higher education institutions with assistance  

[7:2:66,338] through a tax-exempt revenue bond program to reduce the costs of financing academic facilities.   CEFA also may issue bonds,  

[7:3:66,348] notes, and other forms of indebtedness for student loans to support students' costs of higher education.  

[8:0:66,368] CEFA consists of the following five members: the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, and two public  

[8:1:66,379] members appointed by the Governor.  

[9:0:66,409] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[5:0:66,243] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,262] 0940 - BOND FINANCING  

[6:0:66,280] CEFA issues tax-exempt revenue bonds to assist private educational institutions of higher learning to construct educational  

[6:1:66,290] facilities.   Because it is authorized to issue tax-exempt bonds,   CEFA may be able to provide more favorable financing terms  

[6:2:66,300] than might otherwise be obtainable.   CEFA also may include qualifying nonprofit entities as eligible program participants for the  

[6:3:66,310] construction of student and faculty housing.   Bonds issued by CEFA are not a debt, liability, or claim on the faith and credit or  

[6:4:66,320] the taxing power of the State of California or any of its political subdivisions.   The full faith and credit of the participating  

[6:5:66,331] institution is normally pledged to the payment of the bonds.   Bonds issued for this purpose are not subject to the state's "private  

[6:6:66,341] activity" bond ceiling as specified in federal law.  

[7:0:66,359] For purposes of the California Educational Facilities Act, "private college" or "private participating college" means a private  

[7:1:66,369] college that does not restrict the admission of a student based on the student's ethnicity, provided that the financing does not  

[7:2:66,379] violate constitutional provisions.  

[8:0:66,399] "Bond" means bonds, notes, debentures, securities, or other evidences of indebtedness.   This broad definition allows CEFA to  

[8:1:66,409] issue tax-exempt direct or private placement loans for colleges and universities, which may provide greater cost savings than  

[8:2:66,419] these colleges and universities might otherwise realize on a traditional bond financing.  

[9:0:66,439] 0955 - COLLEGE ACCESS TAX CREDIT FUND  

[10:0:66,459] Chapter 367, Statutes of 2014, required CEFA to administer the College Access Tax Credit Fund and allocate and certify the  

[10:1:66,470] tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2014, and before January 1, 2017.   Chapter 22, Statutes of 2015,  

[10:2:66,480] added a requirement that CEFA continue to allocate and certify the tax credits for taxable years beginning on or after January  

[10:3:66,490] 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2018.   Chapter 527, Statutes of 2017, extended CEFA's responsibility to administer the tax  

[10:4:66,500] credits for taxable years beginning on or after January 1, 2017, and before January 1, 2023.
